        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679"><meta property="og:title" content="Yolk–Shell Sb@Void@Graphdiyne Nanoboxes for High-Rate and Long Cycle Life Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/acsnano.2c09679.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Antimony (Sb) has been pursued as a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, it suffers from severe volume expansion during the sodiation–desodiation process. Encapsulating Sb into a carbon matrix can effectively buffer the volume change of Sb. However, the sluggish Na+ diffusion kinetics in traditional carbon shells is still a bottleneck for achieving high-rate performance in Sb/C composite materials. Here we design and synthesize a yolk–shell Sb@Void@graphdiyne (GDY) nanobox (Sb@Void@GDY NB) anode for high-rate and long cycle life SIBs. The intrinsic in-plane cavities in GDY shells offer three-dimensional Na+ transporting channels, enabling fast Na+ diffusion through the GDY shells. Electrochemical kinetics analyses show that the Sb@Void@GDY NBs exhibit faster Na+ transport kinetics than traditional Sb@C NBs. In situ transmission electron microscopy analysis reveals that the hollow structure and the void space between Sb and GDY successfully accommodate the volume change of Sb during cycling, and the plastic GDY shell maintains the structural integrity of NBs. Benefiting from the above structural merits, the Sb@Void@GDY NBs exhibit excellent rate capability and extraordinary cycling stability."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/acsnano.2c09679.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6eda67ebf775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6eda67ebf775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'b8198fec-8ef6-420f-9c11-e8d9faff23fb'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90f6eda67ebf775c-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6eda67ebf775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

2431-2439</span></span></span></div></div></div></div></div><input type="hidden" name="ancac3" id="copyRights" value="/pb-assets/json/journal-info-1738870756703.json"><div <script nonce="90f6eda67ebf775c-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "ancac3";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Yolk–Shell Sb@Void@Graphdiyne Nanoboxes for High-Rate and Long Cycle Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c09679", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Yolk–Shell Sb@Void@Graphdiyne Nanoboxes for High-Rate and Long Cycle Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c09679", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Yolk–Shell Sb@Void@Graphdiyne Nanoboxes for High-Rate and Long Cycle Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c09679", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Yolk–Shell Sb@Void@Graphdiyne Nanoboxes for High-Rate and Long Cycle Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c09679", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Yolk–Shell Sb@Void@Graphdiyne Nanoboxes for High-Rate and Long Cycle Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c09679", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Yolk–Shell Sb@Void@Graphdiyne Nanoboxes for High-Rate and Long Cycle Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c09679", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Yolk–Shell Sb@Void@Graphdiyne Nanoboxes for High-Rate and Long Cycle Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c09679", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Yolk–Shell Sb@Void@Graphdiyne Nanoboxes for High-Rate and Long Cycle Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c09679", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Yolk–Shell Sb@Void@Graphdiyne Nanoboxes for High-Rate and Long Cycle Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c09679", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

R. China</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Yongbin++Wang" data-id="article_author_search">Yongbin Wang</a></span></div></div></div></span></li><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Mingzhe Zhang</span><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Mingzhe Zhang</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">Key Laboratory of Advanced Technologies of Materials (Ministry of Education), School of Materials Science and Engineering, Southwest Jiaotong University, Chengdu, 610031, P. R. R. China</div></div><span class="conrtib-corresp"><strong>*</strong>Email: <a href="mailto:wqyang@swjtu.edu.cn">wqyang@swjtu.edu.cn</a></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Weiqing++Yang" data-id="article_author_search">Weiqing Yang</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" src="/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/orchid-2856f829046fbda55b90e1582edf0e9a.png" alt="Orcid"><a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0001-8828-9862" title="Orcid class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D2431%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYan%2BLiu%252C%2BYue%2BQing%252C%2BBin%2BZhou%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D17%26issueNum%3D3%26contentID%3Dacsnano.2c09679%26title%3DYolk%25E2%2580%2593Shell%2BSb%2540Void%2540Graphdiyne%2BNanoboxes%2Bfor%2BHigh-Rate%2Band%2BLong%2BCycle%2BLife%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D2439%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2023" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/medium/nn2c09679_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c09679&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Antimony (Sb) has been pursued as a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, it suffers from severe volume expansion during the sodiation–desodiation process. Encapsulating Sb into a carbon matrix can effectively buffer the volume change of Sb. However, the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in traditional carbon shells is still a bottleneck for achieving high-rate performance in Sb/C composite materials. Here we design and synthesize a yolk–shell Sb@Void@graphdiyne (GDY) nanobox (Sb@Void@GDY NB) anode for high-rate and long cycle life SIBs. The intrinsic in-plane cavities in GDY shells offer three-dimensional Na<sup>+</sup> transporting channels, enabling fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion through the GDY shells. Electrochemical kinetics analyses show that the Sb@Void@GDY NBs exhibit faster Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics than traditional Sb@C NBs. <i>In situ</i> transmission electron microscopy analysis reveals that the hollow structure and the void space between Sb and GDY successfully accommodate the volume change of Sb during cycling, and the plastic GDY shell maintains the structural integrity of NBs. Benefiting from the above structural merits, the Sb@Void@GDY NBs exhibit excellent rate capability and extraordinary cycling stability.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D2431%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYan%2BLiu%252C%2BYue%2BQing%252C%2BBin%2BZhou%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D17%26issueNum%3D3%26contentID%3Dacsnano.2c09679%26title%3DYolk%25E2%2580%2593Shell%2BSb%2540Void%2540Graphdiyne%2BNanoboxes%2Bfor%2BHigh-Rate%2Band%2BLong%2BCycle%2BLife%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D2439%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffusion" class="article__tags__link">Diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291675&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Stability" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Stability" class="article__tags__link">Stability</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292275&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transmission electron microscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transmission%20electron%20microscopy" class="article__tags__link">Transmission electron microscopy</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Antimony&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Antimony">Antimony</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphdiyne&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphdiyne">graphdiyne</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Yolk%E2%88%92shell&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_yolk%E2%88%92shell">yolk−shell</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Owing to the low cost and wide distribution of Na, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as viable alternatives to lithium-ion batteries for grid-scale energy storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> To date, one of the major obstacles hampering the practical application of SIBs is the lack of appropriate anode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> To address this challenge, many anode materials, mainly including intercalation (e.g., carbonaceous and Ti-based materials), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 6 to 9">(6−9)</a> conversion (e.g., transition metal oxide and transition metal sulfide), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 to 13">(10−13)</a> and alloying compounds (e.g., Sn, Bi, Sb and P), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 14 to 18">(14−18)</a> have been developed. Among them, alloying compounds featuring a high theoretical capacity and relatively low sodiation potential have been regarded as promising anode materials for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Nevertheless, they undergo large volume variation during electrochemical cycling, causing active material pulverization and electrical contact loss, which lead to capacity fading. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To solve the aforementioned issues concerning alloying compounds, researchers have devoted substantial efforts toward improving alloy-based material stability by designing nanostructures, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 21 to 23">(21−23)</a> introducing nonactive/active metal to form alloys, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 24 to 26">(24−26)</a> or forming composites with carbonaceous materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27−29)</a> Among these approaches, using a carbon shell as a buffer layer has been proven to be effective in enhancing electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Particularly, the yolk–shell structure can provide sufficient room to alleviate the volume expansion and prevent solid-electrolyte interface (SEI) film damage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30−32)</a> For example, the Yu group has demonstrated that hollow Sb@C yolk–shell nanospheres afford superior cycling stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> However, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in conventional carbon layers (e.g., amorphous carbon, graphitized carbon, and hard carbon) is sluggish, limiting rate performance in composite materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Additionally, conventional carbon shells are typically formed at high temperatures, which causes volatilization of alloying compounds with low melting points during their synthesis, leading to low active material content in the resulting composites. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref35" aria-label="reference 28 and 35">(28,35)</a> Therefore, developing shell materials with high Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients and relatively low formation temperatures is key to solving the above-mentioned issues.</div><div class="NLM_p">Fortunately, graphdiyne (GDY), a new carbon allotrope, can meet the above-mentioned requirements for shell materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The GDY exhibits a layered two-dimensional planar structure composed of benzene rings connected by butadiyne linkers (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Unlike conventional carbon materials (e.g., graphite, amorphous carbon, carbon nanotubes, and graphene), GDY exhibits intrinsic 18-C cavities uniformly distributed in its plane, allowing metal ions to migrate along the parallel and vertical directions of the carbon plane, beneficial for fast ion transport. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 38 to 40">(38−40)</a> Moreover, GDY can be grown at room temperature via the Glaser-Hay coupling reaction. These features make GDY an ideal shell material for constructing yolk–shell-structured anodes. Therefore, integrating GDY, specifically with a yolk–shell structure, into alloy-type anode materials is crucial in developing high-performance SIB anodes.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/medium/nn2c09679_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of Sb@Void@GDY NB preparation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c09679&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we employed Sb as a typical representative of alloy-based anodes to construct yolk–shell Sb@Void@GDY nanoboxes (NBs) for high-rate and long cycle life SIBs. The Sb@Void@GDY NBs were successfully synthesized via the <i>in situ</i> Glaser–Hay coupling reaction, followed by subsequent thermal reduction and a nanoconfined galvanic replacement reaction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). This yolk–shell Sb@Void@GDY NB architecture demonstrates several key merits for electrochemical Na<sup>+</sup> storage: (i) the interior void of the Sb box and the void between the Sb box and GDY shell provide sufficient room for accommodating Sb volume expansion; (ii) the abundant intrinsic in-plane cavities in GDY facilitate fast ion transport; and (iii) Sb NB aggregation can be effectively prevented owing to the confinement effect of the GDY shells. Because of these structural advantages, the Sb@Void@GDY NB composite exhibits outstanding rate performance and excellent cycling stability.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The preparation procedure for the yolk–shell Sb@Void@GDY NBs is shown in detail in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. In the first step, Cu<sub>2</sub>O nanocubes (NCs) were prepared using a previously reported method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> X-ray diffraction (XRD) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S1">Figure S1</a>), scanning electron microscopy (SEM) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S2a">Figure S2a</a>), and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S2d">Figure S2d</a>) characterizations reveal that the as-prepared Cu<sub>2</sub>O NCs are of pure phase and have a uniform morphology with an average width of ∼450 nm. Subsequently, GDY shells were uniformly grown over Cu<sub>2</sub>O NCs (denoted Cu<sub>2</sub>O@GDY NCs) using hexaethynylbenzene as the precursor through the typical Glaser-Hay reaction (step [i] in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). Raman spectroscopy is a powerful characterization method for detecting carbon–carbon triple bonds (C≡C). The peaks at approximately 1928 and 2167 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be ascribed to the stretching modes of triple bonds (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> In addition, the peaks at approximately 1378 and 1536 cm<sup>–1</sup> correspond to the D and G bands of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon, respectively. The bonding structure was further analyzed via X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). Deconvolution of the high-resolution C 1s spectrum shows four subpeaks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b), which can be assigned to C═C (284.5 eV), C≡C (285.2 eV), C–O (286.2 eV), and C═O (287.3 eV) bonds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 43 to 45">(43−45)</a> Moreover, the integral area ratio of the C═C peak to C≡C peak is calculated to be approximately 1:2, consistent with the bonding structure of GDY. The Cu<sub>2</sub>O@GDY NCs were converted to Cu@Void@GDY NCs (step [ii] in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>) after annealing in Ar/H<sub>2</sub>. The XRD results confirm that Cu<sub>2</sub>O is completely reduced to metallic Cu (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S1">Figure S1</a>). The wrinkled morphology of the GDY shell was well preserved; however, the volume of the inner Cu yolk shrunk relative to original Cu<sub>2</sub>O, producing gaps between the Cu yolk and GDY shell (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S2c, f">Figure S2c, f</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/medium/nn2c09679_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Raman and (b) XPS spectra of Cu<sub>2</sub>O@GDY NCs. (c) SEM image, (d) TEM image, (e) HRTEM image, and (f-h) elemental mapping of yolk–shell Sb@Void@GDY NBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c09679&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">During the yolk–shell Sb@Void@GDY NC fabrication, the Cu@Void@GDY NCs were used as precursors and reductants and were mixed with a SbCl<sub>3</sub> solution to initiate the galvanic replacement process (step [iii] in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). After the replacement reaction, the products inherited the size and wrinkled surfaces of the Cu@Void@GDY NCs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c). The inner solid metallic Cu was converted to a hollow structure, forming a yolk–shell NB, as evidenced by the darker contrast of the edge region than the center region in the Sb yolk (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d). The generation of hollow Sb box can be ascribed to the nanoscale Kirkendall effect. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> The XRD pattern of the yolk–shell sample can be ascribed to pure rhombohedral Sb (JCPDS No. 85–1322) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S1">Figure S1</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e displays the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of the yolk–shell sample. The <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.37 nm corresponds to the (003) plane of Sb. Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) elemental mapping reveals that the Sb box is encapsulated in a carbon shell (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f-h). The Sb content in Sb@Void@GDY NBs is ∼84.3 wt % (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S3">Figure S3</a>) based on thermogravimetric analysis. For comparison, Sb@C NBs were prepared as control samples via hydrothermal treatment, annealing in a reducing atmosphere, and galvanic replacement reaction, as described in detail in our previous study. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The XRD pattern, TEM image, and EDX elemental mapping of Sb@C NBs are shown in the Supporting Information (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figures S4–6">Figures S4–6</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">The Na<sup>+</sup> storage performances of Sb@Void@GDY and Sb@C NB electrodes were evaluated in half cells. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode. Three peaks are observed during the first cathodic scan. The first broad peak at 0.9–1.6 V is probably associated with irreversible reduction of the electrolyte to form the SEI film. The two peaks located at 0.55 and 0.4 V, which are split into three peaks at 0.7, 0.49, and 0.41 V in the following cycles, are attributed to the alloying processes of Sb to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. The difference between the first and the subsequent cathodic CV curves is owing to the different sodiation mechanism of Sb in the first and following cathodic scans, as proposed by Gray et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> In the anodic scan, two oxidation peaks at 0.74 and 0.83 V are associated with the dealloying process of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb to Sb. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b shows the initial four galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) profiles of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode. The Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode shows a relatively low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 45.6%, which should be associated with the irreversible consumption of Na<sup>+</sup> during SEI layer formation and can be substantially improved by adopting many previously reported presodiation strategies. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 50 to 52">(50−52)</a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S7">Figure S7</a> shows the differential capacity curve derived from the second GCD profile in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b. The shape and peak positions of the differential capacity curve are very similar to those of the second CV curve, suggesting that the GCD profiles are in accord with the CV curves.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/medium/nn2c09679_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Initial four CV curves of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Initial four galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Comparison of (c) cycling performances at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (d) rate capability of Sb@Void@GDY and Sb@C NB electrodes. (e) Comparison of long-term cycling stability of Sb@Void@GDY and Sb@C NB electrodes at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c09679&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cycling performances of Sb@Void@GDY and Sb@C NB electrodes are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c. The Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode exhibits much better cycling stability than the Sb@C NB electrode. It delivers a high capacity of 593 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> with almost no capacity loss after 200 cycles, whereas a continuous capacity fading is observed for the Sb@C NB electrode. Such excellent cycling stability of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode is probably due to the yolk–shell architecture, in which ample space between Sb and GDY shells and the inner voids in Sb (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d) allows for the efficient buffering of the volume variation of Sb and preserves the electrode integrity during sodiation/desodiation processes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). To assess the capacity contribution of the GDY shell, hollow GDY NBs (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S8">Figure S8</a>) were prepared via etching the Cu in Cu@GDY NCs with FeCl<sub>3</sub> solution. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S9">Figure S9</a>, the GDY NB electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 295 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Given the GDY content in Sb@Void@GDY NBs is approximately 15.7 wt % (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S3">Figure S3</a>), the reversible capacity contribution of the GDY shell is estimated to be only 7.8%, suggesting that the capacity of the Sb@Void@GDY NBs is mainly contributed by the Sb yolk.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d compares the rate performace of the Sb@Void@GDY NB and Sb@C NB electrodes. The Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode exhibits much higher average reversible capacities compared with the Sb@C electrode at various rates, and this difference becomes more evident with increasing current rates. For example, the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode can deliver a high capacity of 294 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which is two times higher than that of the Sb@C NB electrode (144 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). Additionally, the rate performance of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode is also considerably better than that of many reported Sb-based anodes (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Table S1">Table S1</a>). The enhanced rate capability of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode can be attributed to the introduction of GDY as a protecting shell. The abundant intrinsic in-plane cavities endow GDY with many transport tunnels for Na<sup>+</sup> along the perpendicular direction of the molecular plane, considerably accelerating the Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics. Impressively, the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode also exhibits exceptional long-term cycling performance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e). It maintains a capacity of 325 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (ca. 74% capacity retention) after 8000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which is much better than the Sb@C NB electrode and many previously reported Sb-based anodes (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Table S2">Table S2</a>). The post-mortem SEM analysis shows that the structure of the Sb@Void@GDY NBs is well maintained after 200 cycles, while obvious cracks are observed in the Sb@C NBs, revealing the superior structural stability of the Sb@Void@GDY NBs (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S10">Figure S10</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">We conducted <i>in situ</i> TEM to better understand the real-time structural evolution of the Sb anode upon electrochemical operation and verify the advantages of the yolk–shell structure for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. A nanobattery comprising the Sb@Void@GDY NBs as the working electrode, a piece of Na metal as the counter electrode, and a naturally formed Na<sub>2</sub>O layer on the surface of Na as the electrolyte was constructed, as schematically shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b shows the schematic of structural transformation of the Sb@Void@GDY NBs during the discharge–charge process. A more detailed description of the Na storage mechanism is given in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S11">Figure S11</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c-t shows the microstructure evolution of the Sb yolk during electrochemical cycling (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Movie S1">Movie S1</a>). During the sodiation process, the typical inner Sb cube underwent huge expansion with the side-length expanded by 148% (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S12">Figure S12</a>), corresponding to a volume expansion of about 324%. Benefiting from the well-designed structure with plenty of inner void space, there remained some void space between the inner yolk and the GDY shell after full sodiation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c-i, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Movie S1">Movie S1</a>), contributing to the cycling stability of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode. During the desodiation process, the Sb box shrunk and the initial yolk–shell morphology was preserved after the first dealloy process. Moreover, the Sb@Void@GDY NB structure was maintained without any damage in subsequent cycles, demonstrating the robust structural stability of the Sb@Void@GDY NBs, thereby ensuring extraordinary long-term cycling performance.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/medium/nn2c09679_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Schematic illustration of the <i>in situ</i> TEM device. (b) Schematic of morphological evolution of hollow yolk–shell Sb@Void@GDY NBs during sodiation/desodiation. (c-t) <i>In situ</i> TEM images from time-lapse video of the first four sodiation-desodiation cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c09679&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further reveal the underlying reason for the excellent electrochemical performance of Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode, multiple electrochemical analysis techniques were conducted. First, we adopted the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) to investigate the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) of the Sb@Void@GDY and Sb@C NB electrodes were calculated from the GITT curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S13">Figure S13</a>). During both alloying and dealloying processes, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode are higher than those of the Sb@C NB electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S14">Figure S14</a>), suggesting that Na<sup>+</sup> transport in the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode is faster than in the Sb@C NB electrode. In addition, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of GDY NB electrode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S15">Figure S15</a>) are approximately 2 orders of magnitude higher than that of Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode, implying the fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in the GDY shell.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/medium/nn2c09679_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) GITT curve of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode. (b) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients estimated from (a). (c) EIS curves of the Sb@Void@GDY and Sb@C NB electrodes after different cycles. (d) CV curves of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode at varying scan rates. (e) Plots of log(i) as a function of log(ν) for the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode. (f) Diagram of capacitive and diffusion-controlled contributions for the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode at various sweep rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c09679&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was also performed to study the charge transport kinetics of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode. The Nyquist plots (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c) were fitted with a simplified equivalent circuit model (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S16">Figure S16</a>) and the corresponding fitting data were displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Table S3">Table S3</a>. Comparing the fitting results in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Table S3">Table S3</a>, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the Sb@Void@GDY NB is more stable than that of the Sb@C NB electrode, indicating that the GDY shell can enhance the structural stability of the composite electrode and afford a stable SEI layer, thus providing excellent cycling stability. To analyze the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of the aforementioned electrodes, the Warburg factor (σ) was estimated from the linear fitting of the low-frequency region of the <i>Z′−ω</i><sup><i>–</i>1/2</sup> plot (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S17">Figure S17</a>). The Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode shows a much lower σ than the Sb@C NB electrode, implying that the GDY shell can facilitate fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, thus enabling superior rate capability.</div><div class="NLM_p">CV was performed at various sweep rates to illuminate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage dynamics in Sb@Void@GDY NB electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d). The diffusion and capacitance contributions to the sodium storage capacity of the material can be deduced from the CV curves. Assuming the anodic and cathodic currents obey the following equation: <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><math display="inline"><mi>i</mi><mo>=</mo><mi>a</mi><msup><mrow><mi>ν</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>b</mi></mrow></msup><mo>↔</mo><mi>log</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>i</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>=</mo><mi>b</mi><mi>log</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>ν</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>+</mo><mi>log</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>a</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></math>, where <i>i</i> and ν represent the peak current and the corresponding scanning rate, respectively. The parameter <i>b</i> was obtained by linear fitting the log(<i>i</i>)–log(ν) plots (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e). When the <i>b</i> value approaches 1.0, the charge storage mechanism of the electrode is governed by a capacitive-controlled process, whereas a diffusion-controlled process dominates the system, if the <i>b</i> value is close to 0.5. The <i>b</i> values of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode are 0.76 (peak A1), 0.93 (peak C1), and 0.90 (peak C2), implying the reaction dynamics of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode is dominated by a fast capacitive-controlled process. Diffusion- and capacitive-controlled capacities can be separated according to the following equation:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mi>ν</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msup><mrow><mi>ν</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>0.5</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"></div></div>where <i>i</i>, ν, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν, and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>ν<sup>0.5</sup> are the total current response during CV tests, CV scan rate, capacitive-controlled contribution, and diffusion-controlled contribution, respectively. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f, the capacitive contribution of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode increases from 58% to 83% when the scan rate increases from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, suggesting that the surface capacitive process dominates the charge storage process of the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode, especially at high rates. Moreover, the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode presents a higher capacitive-controlled contribution than the Sb@C NB electrode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S18">Figure S18</a>), indicating that the GDY shell contributes to the surface capacitive capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">Inspired by the outstanding Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties of the Sb@Void@GDY NBs, we assembled a full cell using the Sb@Void@GDY NBs as an anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as a cathode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a). The electrochemical performance of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S19">Figure S19</a>. The full cell delivers reversible capacities of 557, 520, 469, 399, 300, and 216 mAh g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>, respectively. Moreover, the full cell achieves a high capacity retention of 75% and preserves a capacity of 354 mAh g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> after 500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c). As a demonstration, the charged full cell can successfully lit up 84 light-emitting diodes with a pattern of “Sb@GDY” (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d, a high specific power of 4 kWh kg<sup>–1</sup> and specific energy of 235 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> are achieved for the full cell, highlighting the practical application potential of the Sb@Void@GDY NBs in Na<sup>+</sup> full cells.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/medium/nn2c09679_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Schematic showing the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>∥Sb@Void@GDY NB full cell. (b) Charge–discharge curves of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>∥Sb@Void@GDY NB full cell at different current densities. (c) Cycling performance of the full cell at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> and digital photo of a “Sb@GDY” shaped LED panel lit by a charged full cell. The capacity and current density in (b-c) is based on the mass of the anode. (d) Ragone plot and average discharge voltage at different current densities of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>∥Sb@Void@GDY NB full cell. The energy density and power density are calculated based on the total mass of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Sb@Void@GDY NBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/asset/images/large/nn2c09679_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c09679&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have developed yolk–shell structured Sb@Void@GDY NBs via a Glaser-Hay coupling reaction combined with a galvanic replacement reaction. Comprehensive characterizations revel that the void space in the yolk–shell Sb@Void@GDY NBs successfully tolerates the volume changes of the inner Sb yolk and the special GDY shell with intrinsic in-plane cavities promotes the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. As a result, the Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode exhibits enhanced rate capability and cycling stability compared with conventional Sb@C NB electrode. The methodology of constructing yolk–shell structured electrodes with a GDY shell layer can potentially be extended to other materials for building high-performance electrodes.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Preparation of Cu<sub>2</sub>O NCs</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In a typical procedure, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> 7.5 mmol CuSO<sub>4</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O and 2.5 mmol sodium citrate were dissolved in 400 mL ultrapure water. Then, 100 mL of 1.25 M NaOH was mixed with the aforementioned solution under stirring for 3 min. Afterward, 250 mL of 0.03 M ascorbic acid was added to the obtained solution under stirring for 5 min. The products were collected via centrifugation after aging for 2 h at room temperature. The obtained precipitate was washed with ultrapure water and ethanol several times. Finally, the obtained Cu<sub>2</sub>O NCs were dried at 50 °C under vacuum for 6 h.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Synthesis of Cu<sub>2</sub>O@GDY NCs</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Hexaethynylbenzene (HEB) was synthesized and purified using a previously reported approach. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> The as-prepared Cu<sub>2</sub>O NCs were dispersed in acetone under ultrasonication for 1 h at a concentration of 5 mg mL<sup>–1</sup> and then mixed with a HEB-pyridine solution (1 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>) in a glass bottle under gentle stirring to form a homogeneous suspension. The molar ratio of Cu<sub>2</sub>O to HEB in the suspension was controlled at 30:1. The suspension was kept in the dark, stirred for 24 h, and then left to stand for 48 h. The Cu<sub>2</sub>O@GDY NCs were collected from the suspension via centrifugation. The obtained precipitate was redispersed in ethanol and precipitated by centrifugation several times. The final precipitate was vacuum-dried for 6 h.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Synthesis of Cu@GDY NCs</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Cu<sub>2</sub>O@GDY NCs were placed in a quartz boat and heated to 400 °C in an Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (94:6 volume) atmosphere for 5 h in a tube furnace. The obtained sample was kept in an inert atmosphere to avoid oxidation.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Synthesis of Sb@Void@GDY NBs</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO) was dried over freshly activated 3-Å molecular sieves for a week before use. Tetrabutylammonium tetrafluoroborate (TBABF<sub>4</sub>) was dried under vacuum at 100 °C before use. All the synthetic procedures for synthesizing Sb@Void@GDY NBs were performed in an Ar-filled glovebox. In a typical synthesis, 360 mg Cu@GDY NCs was dispersed in 36 mL DMSO under sonication for 30 min, forming a reddish-brown suspension. The obtained suspension was mixed with 230 mL 0.05 M SbCl<sub>3</sub>-0.1 M TBABF<sub>4</sub>-DMSO solution under stirring and the resulting mixture was stirred gently for 72 h. The resulting solution was precipitated by centrifugation and the supernatant was discarded. The precipitate was redispersed in ethanol and precipitated by centrifugation several times. Finally, the precipitate was vacuum-dried at 50 °C for 6 h.</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Synthesis of Sb@C NBs</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In detail, 120 mg glucose and 60 mg Cu<sub>2</sub>O NCs were added into 60 mL ultrapure water under sonication, forming a uniform suspension. Then, the suspension was hydrothermally treated at 190 °C for 10 h. The resulting reaction solution was precipitated by centrifugation. The obtained precipitate was first washed with water two times and then washed with ethanol. After drying under vacuum, the product was heated to 500 °C for 3h under Ar/H<sub>2</sub> atmosphere to gain Cu@C NCs. Afterward, 32 mg Cu@C NCs was dispersed in 6.4 mL DMSO under sonication. The obtained suspension was poured into 100 mL 0.01 M SbCl<sub>3</sub>-0.1 M TBABF<sub>4</sub>-DMSO solution. After stirring for 3 h, the reaction solution was allowed to stand for 4 days. The resulting reaction solution was centrifugated to collect precipitate. The Sb@C NBs were washed by ethanol three times before drying in a vacuum oven for 6 h.</div></div><div id="sec4_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Powder XRD measurements were conducted using a PANalytical X’Pert powder diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation. The morphologies of samples were observed via SEM (JSM-7800 Prime, JEOL), TEM (HT7700, Hitachi), and HRTEM (Libra 200FE, Zeiss). Raman spectra were acquired from a Horiba XploRA Raman spectrometer with a 532 nm excitation laser. XPS was performed using an ESCALAB 250Xi spectrometer (Thermo Scientific). <i>In situ</i> TEM test was conducted on a JEOL 2100F TEM.</div></div><div id="sec4_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrodes with active material mass loading of approximately 1 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> were prepared by coating a slurry containing 70 wt % as-prepared material, 20 wt % Ketjen black, and 10 wt % sodium carboxymethyl cellulose onto copper foils. The electrodes were dried under vacuum at 50 °C for 2 h, pressed with a roll squeezer, and cut into 12 mm disks. The electrochemical performance of Sb@Void@GDY NB and Sb@C NB electrodes was investigated using CR2032 coin-type half cells, in which the working electrode was paired with a Na counter electrode. The separator was a glass fiber membrane (Whatman) with a diameter of 19 mm. The electrolyte was a 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate (1:1 by vol) containing 5 vol % fluoroethylene carbonate. For the full cells, the cathode was prepared using procedures similar to those used for preparing the Sb@Void@GDY NB anode, except that the Sb@Void@GDY NBs were replaced by commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and the copper foils were replaced by aluminum foils. Both the cathode and the anode were precycled before full cell assembly to minimize irreversible capacity loss. The capacity ratio of the cathode/anode was approximately 1.05:1. EIS and CV measurements were performed on a 302N electrochemical workstation (Autolab). Galvanostatic cycling measurements were conducted using a CT3001A battery tester (LAND).</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i15">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Supplementary XRD patterns, SEM and TEM images of Cu<sub>2</sub>O NCs, Cu<sub>2</sub>O@GDY NCs, Cu@Void@GDY NCs, Sb@Void@GDY NBs, GDY NBs, and post-mortem electrode; TGA results of the Sb@Void@GDY NBs; EDX elemental mapping images of Sb@C NB; differential capacity curve for Sb@Void@GDY NB electrode; cycling performance of GDY NB electrode; schematic illustration of the Na storage mechanism; kinetic analysis of electrochemical peformances for samples; cycling performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode; tables for comparison of sodium storage performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/suppl_file/nn2c09679_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li><li><p class="inline">Movie S1: In situ TEM cycling video of Sb@Void@GDY NBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/suppl_file/nn2c09679_si_002.mp4" class="ext-link">MP4</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/suppl_file/nn2c09679_si_001.pdf">nn2c09679_si_001.pdf (1.68 MB)</a></li><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c09679/suppl_file/nn2c09679_si_002.mp4">nn2c09679_si_002.mp4 (54.49 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.2c09679" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">
